<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">William Shakespeare</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
</div>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about the poet and playwright. For other persons of the same name, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/William_Shakespeare_(disambiguation)" title="William Shakespeare (disambiguation)">William Shakespeare (disambiguation)</a>. For other uses of "Shakespeare", see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Shakespeare_(disambiguation)" title="Shakespeare (disambiguation)">Shakespeare (disambiguation)</a>.</div>

<p><b>William Shakespeare</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="/ʃ/: 'sh' in 'shy'">ʃ</span><span title="/eɪ/: 'a' in 'face'">eɪ</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="'p' in 'pie'">p</span><span title="/ɪər/: 'ear' in 'near'">ɪər</span></span>/</a></span></span>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> 26 April 1564 (<a href="/wiki/Baptism" title="Baptism">baptised</a>) – 23 April 1616)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dates_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-dates-2">[nb 1]</a></sup> was an English <a href="/wiki/Poet" title="Poet">poet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Playwright" title="Playwright">playwright</a> and actor, widely regarded as the greatest writer in the <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English language</a> and the world's pre-eminent dramatist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harvnb1_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Harvnb1-3">[2]</a></sup> He is often called England's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_national_poets" title="List of national poets">national poet</a> and the "Bard of Avon".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-national-cult_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-national-cult-5">[nb 2]</a></sup> His extant works, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shakespeare%27s_collaborations" title="Shakespeare's collaborations">collaborations</a>, consist of approximately <a href="/wiki/Shakespeare%27s_plays" title="Shakespeare's plays">38 plays</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-exact-figures_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-exact-figures-6">[nb 3]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Shakespeare%27s_sonnets" title="Shakespeare's sonnets">154 sonnets</a>, two long <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Narrative_poem" title="Narrative poem">narrative poems</a> and a few other verses, some of uncertain authorship. His plays have been translated into every major <a href="/wiki/Modern_language" title="Modern language">living language</a> and are performed more often than those of any other playwright.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p>Shakespeare was born and brought up in <a href="/wiki/Stratford-upon-Avon" title="Stratford-upon-Avon">Stratford-upon-Avon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Warwickshire" title="Warwickshire">Warwickshire</a>. At the age of 18, he married <a href="/wiki/Anne_Hathaway_(wife_of_Shakespeare)" title="Anne Hathaway (wife of Shakespeare)">Anne Hathaway</a>, with whom he had three children: <a href="/wiki/Susanna_Hall" title="Susanna Hall">Susanna</a> and twins <a href="/wiki/Hamnet_Shakespeare" title="Hamnet Shakespeare">Hamnet</a> and <a href="/wiki/Judith_Quiney" title="Judith Quiney">Judith</a>. Sometime between 1585 and 1592, he began a successful career in London as an actor, writer, and part-owner of a <a href="/wiki/Playing_company" title="Playing company">playing company</a> called the <a href="/wiki/Lord_Chamberlain%27s_Men" title="Lord Chamberlain's Men">Lord Chamberlain's Men</a>, later known as the <a href="/wiki/King%27s_Men_(playing_company)" title="King's Men (playing company)">King's Men</a>. At age 49 around 1613, he appears to have retired to Stratford, where he died three years later. Few records of Shakespeare's private life survive, which has stimulated considerable speculation about such matters as <a href="/wiki/Portraits_of_Shakespeare" title="Portraits of Shakespeare">his physical appearance</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sexuality_of_William_Shakespeare" title="Sexuality of William Shakespeare">sexuality</a>, <a href="/wiki/Religious_views_of_William_Shakespeare" title="Religious views of William Shakespeare">religious beliefs</a> and whether the works attributed to him were <a href="/wiki/Shakespeare_authorship_question" title="Shakespeare authorship question">written by others</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>Shakespeare produced most of his known work between 1589 and 1613.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-play-dates_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-play-dates-10">[nb 4]</a></sup> His early plays were primarily <a href="/wiki/Shakespearean_comedy" title="Shakespearean comedy">comedies</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shakespearean_history" title="Shakespearean history">histories</a>, which are regarded as some of the best work ever produced in these genres. He then wrote mainly <a href="/wiki/Shakespearean_tragedy" title="Shakespearean tragedy">tragedies</a> until about 1608, including <i><a href="/wiki/Hamlet" title="Hamlet">Hamlet</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Othello" title="Othello">Othello</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/King_Lear" title="King Lear">King Lear</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Macbeth" title="Macbeth">Macbeth</a></i>, considered some of the finest works in the English language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harvnb1_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-Harvnb1-3">[2]</a></sup> In his last phase, he wrote <a href="/wiki/Tragicomedy" title="Tragicomedy">tragicomedies</a>, also known as <a href="/wiki/Shakespeare%27s_late_romances" title="Shakespeare's late romances">romances</a> and collaborated with other playwrights.</p>
<p>Many of his plays were published in editions of varying quality and accuracy during his lifetime. However, in 1623 <a href="/wiki/John_Heminges" title="John Heminges">John Heminges</a> and <a href="/wiki/Henry_Condell" title="Henry Condell">Henry Condell</a>, two friends and fellow actors of Shakespeare, published a more definitive text known as the <a href="/wiki/First_Folio" title="First Folio">First Folio</a>, a posthumous collected edition of his dramatic works that included all but two of the plays now recognised as Shakespeare's.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NortonAnth1_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-NortonAnth1-11">[7]</a></sup> It was prefaced with a poem by <a href="/wiki/Ben_Jonson" title="Ben Jonson">Ben Jonson</a>, in which Shakespeare is hailed, presciently, as "not of an age, but for all time".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NortonAnth1_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-NortonAnth1-11">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the 20th and 21st centuries, his works have been repeatedly adapted and rediscovered by new movements in scholarship and performance. His plays remain highly popular and are constantly studied, performed and reinterpreted in diverse cultural and political contexts throughout the world.</p>
<p></p>

<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-15"><a href="#Speculation_about_Shakespeare"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">Speculation about Shakespeare</span></a>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Life">Life</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Shakespeare%27s_life" title="Shakespeare's life">Shakespeare's life</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span></h3>
<p>William Shakespeare was the son of <a href="/wiki/John_Shakespeare" title="John Shakespeare">John Shakespeare</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Alderman" title="Alderman">alderman</a> and a successful glover originally from <a href="/wiki/Snitterfield" title="Snitterfield">Snitterfield</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mary_Shakespeare" title="Mary Shakespeare">Mary Arden</a>, the daughter of an affluent landowning farmer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[8]</a></sup> He was born in <a href="/wiki/Stratford-upon-Avon" title="Stratford-upon-Avon">Stratford-upon-Avon</a> and baptised there on 26 April 1564. His actual date of birth remains unknown, but is traditionally observed on 23 April, <a href="/wiki/Saint_George%27s_Day" title="Saint George's Day">Saint George's Day</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[9]</a></sup> This date, which can be traced back to an 18th-century scholar's mistake, has proved appealing to biographers because Shakespeare died on 23 April 1616.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[10]</a></sup> He was the third child of eight and the eldest surviving son.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[11]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:William_Shakespeares_birthplace,_Stratford-upon-Avon_26l2007.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="435" data-file-width="800" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/William_Shakespeares_birthplace%2C_Stratford-upon-Avon_26l2007.jpg/220px-William_Shakespeares_birthplace%2C_Stratford-upon-Avon_26l2007.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/William_Shakespeares_birthplace%2C_Stratford-upon-Avon_26l2007.jpg/330px-William_Shakespeares_birthplace%2C_Stratford-upon-Avon_26l2007.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/William_Shakespeares_birthplace%2C_Stratford-upon-Avon_26l2007.jpg/440px-William_Shakespeares_birthplace%2C_Stratford-upon-Avon_26l2007.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:William_Shakespeares_birthplace,_Stratford-upon-Avon_26l2007.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
John Shakespeare's house, believed to be <a href="/wiki/Shakespeare%27s_Birthplace" title="Shakespeare's Birthplace">Shakespeare's birthplace</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Stratford-upon-Avon" title="Stratford-upon-Avon">Stratford-upon-Avon</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Although no attendance records for the period survive, most biographers agree that Shakespeare was probably educated at the <a href="/wiki/King_Edward_VI_School,_Stratford-upon-Avon" title="King Edward VI School, Stratford-upon-Avon">King's New School</a> in Stratford,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[12]</a></sup> a free school chartered in 1553,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[13]</a></sup> about a quarter-mile (400 m) from his home. <a href="/wiki/Grammar_school" title="Grammar school">Grammar schools</a> varied in quality during the Elizabethan era, but grammar school curricula were largely similar: the basic <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> text was standardised by royal decree,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[14]</a></sup> and the school would have provided an intensive education in grammar based upon Latin <a href="/wiki/Classics" title="Classics">classical</a> authors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>At the age of 18, Shakespeare married 26-year-old <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anne_Hathaway_(Shakespeare%27s_wife)" title="Anne Hathaway (Shakespeare's wife)">Anne Hathaway</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Consistory_court" title="Consistory court">consistory court</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Anglican_Diocese_of_Worcester" title="Anglican Diocese of Worcester">Diocese of Worcester</a> issued a marriage licence on 27 November 1582. The next day, two of Hathaway's neighbours posted bonds guaranteeing that no lawful claims impeded the marriage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[16]</a></sup> The ceremony may have been arranged in some haste since the Worcester <a href="/wiki/Chancellor" title="Chancellor">chancellor</a> allowed the <a href="/wiki/Banns_of_marriage" title="Banns of marriage">marriage banns</a> to be read once instead of the usual three times,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[17]</a></sup> and six months after the marriage Anne gave birth to a daughter, <a href="/wiki/Susanna_Hall" title="Susanna Hall">Susanna</a>, baptised 26 May 1583.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[18]</a></sup> Twins, son <a href="/wiki/Hamnet_Shakespeare" title="Hamnet Shakespeare">Hamnet</a> and daughter <a href="/wiki/Judith_Quiney" title="Judith Quiney">Judith</a>, followed almost two years later and were baptised 2 February 1585.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[19]</a></sup> Hamnet died of unknown causes at the age of 11 and was buried 11 August 1596.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[20]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Shakespeare_coat-of-arms.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="330" data-file-width="278" height="261" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Shakespeare_coat-of-arms.jpg/220px-Shakespeare_coat-of-arms.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/54/Shakespeare_coat-of-arms.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Shakespeare_coat-of-arms.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Shakespeare's coat of arms, as it appears on the rough draft of the application to grant a coat-of-arms to John Shakespeare. It features a spear as a <a href="/wiki/Canting_arms" title="Canting arms">pun</a> on the family name.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[nb 5]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After the birth of the twins, Shakespeare left few historical traces until he is mentioned as part of the London theatre scene in 1592. The exception is the appearance of his name in the "complaints bill" of a law case before the Queen's Bench court at Westminster dated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Michaelmas_Term" title="Michaelmas Term">Michaelmas Term</a> 1588 and 9 October 1589.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[21]</a></sup> Scholars refer to the years between 1585 and 1592 as Shakespeare's "lost years".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[22]</a></sup> Biographers attempting to account for this period have reported many <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/apocryphal" title="wikt:apocryphal">apocryphal</a> stories. <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_Rowe_(writer)" title="Nicholas Rowe (writer)">Nicholas Rowe</a>, Shakespeare's first biographer, recounted a Stratford legend that Shakespeare fled the town for London to escape prosecution for deer <a href="/wiki/Poaching" title="Poaching">poaching</a> in the estate of local squire <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Lucy" title="Thomas Lucy">Thomas Lucy</a>. Shakespeare is also supposed to have taken his revenge on Lucy by writing a scurrilous ballad about him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[23]</a></sup> Another 18th-century story has Shakespeare starting his theatrical career minding the horses of theatre patrons in London.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[24]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/John_Aubrey" title="John Aubrey">John Aubrey</a> reported that Shakespeare had been a country schoolmaster.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[25]</a></sup> Some 20th-century scholars have suggested that Shakespeare may have been employed as a schoolmaster by Alexander Hoghton of <a href="/wiki/Lancashire" title="Lancashire">Lancashire</a>, a Catholic landowner who named a certain "William Shakeshafte" in his will.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[26]</a></sup> Little evidence substantiates such stories other than <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/hearsay" title="wikt:hearsay">hearsay</a> collected after his death, and Shakeshafte was a common name in the Lancashire area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[27]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="London_and_theatrical_career">London and theatrical career</span></h3>
<p>It is not known definitively when Shakespeare began writing, but contemporary allusions and records of performances show that several of his plays were on the London stage by 1592.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[28]</a></sup> By then, he was sufficiently known in London to be attacked in print by the playwright <a href="/wiki/Robert_Greene_(dramatist)" title="Robert Greene (dramatist)">Robert Greene</a> in his <i><a href="/wiki/Greene%27s_Groats-Worth_of_Wit" title="Greene's Groats-Worth of Wit">Groats-Worth of Wit</a></i>:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>... there is an upstart Crow, beautified with our feathers, that with his <i>Tiger's heart wrapped in a Player's hide</i>, supposes he is as well able to bombast out a blank verse as the best of you: and being an absolute <i>Johannes factotum</i>, is in his own conceit the only Shake-scene in a country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[29]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Scholars differ on the exact meaning of Greene's words,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SG213_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-SG213-35">[30]</a></sup> but most agree that Greene was accusing Shakespeare of reaching above his rank in trying to match such university-educated writers as <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Marlowe" title="Christopher Marlowe">Christopher Marlowe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Nashe" title="Thomas Nashe">Thomas Nashe</a>, and Greene himself (the so-called <a href="/wiki/University_Wits" title="University Wits">"university wits"</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[31]</a></sup> The italicised phrase parodying the line "Oh, tiger's heart wrapped in a woman's hide" from Shakespeare's <i><a href="/wiki/Henry_VI,_Part_3" title="Henry VI, Part 3">Henry VI, Part 3</a></i>, along with the pun "Shake-scene", clearly identify Shakespeare as Greene's target. As used here, <i><a href="/wiki/Jack_of_all_trades,_master_of_none" title="Jack of all trades, master of none">Johannes Factotum</a></i> ("Jack of all trades") refers to a second-rate tinkerer with the work of others, rather than the more common "universal genius".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SG213_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-SG213-35">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[32]</a></sup></p>
<p>Greene's attack is the earliest surviving mention of Shakespeare's work in the theatre. Biographers suggest that his career may have begun any time from the mid-1580s to just before Greene's remarks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[33]</a></sup> After 1594, Shakespeare's plays were performed only by the <a href="/wiki/Lord_Chamberlain%27s_Men" title="Lord Chamberlain's Men">Lord Chamberlain's Men</a>, a company owned by a group of players, including Shakespeare, that soon became the leading <a href="/wiki/Playing_company" title="Playing company">playing company</a> in London.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[34]</a></sup> After the death of <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_I_of_England" title="Elizabeth I of England">Queen Elizabeth</a> in 1603, the company was awarded a royal patent by the new <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/James_I_of_England" title="James I of England">King James I</a>, and changed its name to the <a href="/wiki/King%27s_Men_(playing_company)" title="King's Men (playing company)">King's Men</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[35]</a></sup></p>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; padding: 10px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>
<div class="poem">
<p>"All the world's a stage,<br/>
and all the men and women merely players:<br/>
they have their exits and their entrances;<br/>
and one man in his time plays many parts ..."</p>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: left;">—<i><a href="/wiki/As_You_Like_It" title="As You Like It">As You Like It</a></i>, Act II, Scene 7, 139–42<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[36]</a></sup></cite></div>
<p>In 1599, a partnership of members of the company built their own theatre on the south bank of the <a href="/wiki/River_Thames" title="River Thames">River Thames</a>, which they named the <a href="/wiki/Globe_Theatre" title="Globe Theatre">Globe</a>. In 1608, the partnership also took over the <a href="/wiki/Blackfriars_Theatre" title="Blackfriars Theatre">Blackfriars indoor theatre</a>. Extant records of Shakespeare's property purchases and investments indicate that his association with the company made him a wealthy man,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[37]</a></sup> and in 1597, he bought the second-largest house in Stratford, <a href="/wiki/New_Place" title="New Place">New Place</a>, and in 1605, invested in a share of the parish <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tithes" title="Tithes">tithes</a> in Stratford.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[38]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some of Shakespeare's plays were published in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quarto_(binding)" title="Quarto (binding)">quarto</a> editions, beginning in 1594, and by 1598, his name had become a selling point and began to appear on the <a href="/wiki/Title_page" title="Title page">title pages</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[39]</a></sup> Shakespeare continued to act in his own and other plays after his success as a playwright. The 1616 edition of <a href="/wiki/Ben_Jonson" title="Ben Jonson">Ben Jonson</a>'s <i>Works</i> names him on the cast lists for <i><a href="/wiki/Every_Man_in_His_Humour" title="Every Man in His Humour">Every Man in His Humour</a></i> (1598) and <i><a href="/wiki/Sejanus_His_Fall" title="Sejanus His Fall">Sejanus His Fall</a></i> (1603).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[40]</a></sup> The absence of his name from the 1605 cast list for Jonson's <i><a href="/wiki/Volpone" title="Volpone">Volpone</a></i> is taken by some scholars as a sign that his acting career was nearing its end.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[41]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/First_Folio" title="First Folio">First Folio</a> of 1623, however, lists Shakespeare as one of "the Principal Actors in all these Plays", some of which were first staged after <i>Volpone</i>, although we cannot know for certain which roles he played.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[42]</a></sup> In 1610, <a href="/wiki/John_Davies_of_Hereford" title="John Davies of Hereford">John Davies of Hereford</a> wrote that "good Will" played "kingly" roles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[43]</a></sup> In 1709, Rowe passed down a tradition that Shakespeare played the ghost of Hamlet's father.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[44]</a></sup> Later traditions maintain that he also played Adam in <i><a href="/wiki/As_You_Like_It" title="As You Like It">As You Like It</a></i>, and the Chorus in <i><a href="/wiki/Henry_V_(play)" title="Henry V (play)">Henry V</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[45]</a></sup> though scholars doubt the sources of that information.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>Throughout his career, Shakespeare divided his time between London and Stratford. In 1596, the year before he bought New Place as his family home in Stratford, Shakespeare was living in the parish of St. Helen's, <a href="/wiki/Bishopsgate" title="Bishopsgate">Bishopsgate</a>, north of the River Thames.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHales1904401.E2.80.9302_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHales1904401.E2.80.9302-52">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[48]</a></sup> He moved across the river to <a href="/wiki/Southwark" title="Southwark">Southwark</a> by 1599, the same year his company constructed the Globe Theatre there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHales1904401.E2.80.9302_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHales1904401.E2.80.9302-52">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[49]</a></sup> By 1604, he had moved north of the river again, to an area north of <a href="/wiki/St_Paul%27s_Cathedral" title="St Paul's Cathedral">St Paul's Cathedral</a> with many fine houses. There, he rented rooms from a French <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Huguenot" title="Huguenot">Huguenot</a> named Christopher Mountjoy, a maker of ladies' wigs and other headgear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[50]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_years_and_death">Later years and death</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ShakespeareMonument_cropped.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1309" data-file-width="537" height="414" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/ShakespeareMonument_cropped.jpg/170px-ShakespeareMonument_cropped.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/ShakespeareMonument_cropped.jpg/255px-ShakespeareMonument_cropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/ShakespeareMonument_cropped.jpg/340px-ShakespeareMonument_cropped.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ShakespeareMonument_cropped.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Shakespeare%27s_funerary_monument" title="Shakespeare's funerary monument">Shakespeare's funerary monument</a> in Stratford-upon-Avon</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Nicholas_Rowe_(writer)" title="Nicholas Rowe (writer)">Rowe</a> was the first biographer to record the tradition, repeated by <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Johnson" title="Samuel Johnson">Johnson</a>, that Shakespeare retired to Stratford "some years before his death".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-56">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWood1806ix.E2.80.93x.2C_lxxii_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWood1806ix.E2.80.93x.2C_lxxii-57">[52]</a></sup> He was still working as an actor in London in 1608; in an answer to the sharers' petition in 1635, <a href="/wiki/Cuthbert_Burbage" title="Cuthbert Burbage">Cuthbert Burbage</a> stated that after purchasing the lease of the <a href="/wiki/Blackfriars_Theatre" title="Blackfriars Theatre">Blackfriars Theatre</a> in 1608 from <a href="/wiki/Henry_Evans_(theatre)" title="Henry Evans (theatre)">Henry Evans</a>, the King's Men "placed men players" there, "which were <a href="/wiki/John_Heminges" title="John Heminges">Heminges</a>, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Condell" title="Henry Condell">Condell</a>, Shakespeare, etc.".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmith1964558_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmith1964558-58">[53]</a></sup> However, it is perhaps relevant that the <a href="/wiki/Bubonic_plague" title="Bubonic plague">bubonic plague</a> raged in London throughout 1609.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAckroyd2006477_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAckroyd2006477-59">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarroll1991179.E2.80.9382_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarroll1991179.E2.80.9382-60">[55]</a></sup> The London public playhouses were repeatedly closed during extended outbreaks of the plague (a total of over 60 months closure between May 1603 and February 1610),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[56]</a></sup> which meant there was often no acting work. Retirement from all work was uncommon at that time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[57]</a></sup> Shakespeare continued to visit London during the years 1611–1614.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-56">[51]</a></sup> In 1612, he was called as a witness in <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bellott_v._Mountjoy" title="Bellott v. Mountjoy">Bellott v. Mountjoy</a></i>, a court case concerning the marriage settlement of Mountjoy's daughter, Mary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[58]</a></sup> In March 1613, he bought a <a href="/wiki/Gatehouse" title="Gatehouse">gatehouse</a> in the former <a href="/wiki/Blackfriars,_London" title="Blackfriars, London">Blackfriars</a> priory;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[59]</a></sup> and from November 1614, he was in London for several weeks with his son-in-law, <a href="/wiki/John_Hall_(physician)" title="John Hall (physician)">John Hall</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[60]</a></sup> After 1610, Shakespeare wrote fewer plays, and none are attributed to him after 1613.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[61]</a></sup> His last three plays were collaborations, probably with <a href="/wiki/John_Fletcher_(playwright)" title="John Fletcher (playwright)">John Fletcher</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[62]</a></sup> who succeeded him as the house playwright of the King's Men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[63]</a></sup></p>
<p>Shakespeare died on 23 April 1616, at the age of 52.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[64]</a></sup> He died within a month of signing his will, a document which he begins by describing himself as being in "perfect health". No extant contemporary source explains how or why he died. Half a century later, <a href="/wiki/John_Ward_(vicar)" title="John Ward (vicar)">John Ward</a>, the vicar of Stratford, wrote in his notebook: "Shakespeare, Drayton, and Ben Jonson had a merry meeting and, it seems, drank too hard, for Shakespeare died of a fever there contracted",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[66]</a></sup> not an impossible scenario since Shakespeare knew Jonson and <a href="/wiki/Michael_Drayton" title="Michael Drayton">Drayton</a>. Of the tributes from fellow authors, one refers to his relatively sudden death: "We wondered, Shakespeare, that thou went'st so soon/From the world's stage to the grave's tiring room."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[67]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Holy_Trinity_Church,_Stratford-upon-Avon.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2774" data-file-width="3592" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Holy_Trinity_Church%2C_Stratford-upon-Avon.jpg/220px-Holy_Trinity_Church%2C_Stratford-upon-Avon.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Holy_Trinity_Church%2C_Stratford-upon-Avon.jpg/330px-Holy_Trinity_Church%2C_Stratford-upon-Avon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Holy_Trinity_Church%2C_Stratford-upon-Avon.jpg/440px-Holy_Trinity_Church%2C_Stratford-upon-Avon.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Holy_Trinity_Church,_Stratford-upon-Avon.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Church_of_the_Holy_Trinity,_Stratford-upon-Avon" title="Church of the Holy Trinity, Stratford-upon-Avon">Holy Trinity Church, Stratford-upon-Avon</a>, where Shakespeare was baptised and is buried</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>He was survived by his wife and two daughters. Susanna had married a physician, John Hall, in 1607,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[68]</a></sup> and Judith had married <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Quiney" title="Thomas Quiney">Thomas Quiney</a>, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vintner" title="Vintner">vintner</a>, two months before Shakespeare's death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[69]</a></sup> Shakespeare signed his last will and testament on 25 March 1616; the following day, his new son-in-law, Thomas Quiney was found guilty of fathering an illegitimate son by Margaret Wheeler, who had died during childbirth. Thomas was ordered by the church court to do public penance, which would have caused much shame and embarrassment for the Shakespeare family.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[70]</a></sup></p>
<p>Shakespeare bequeathed the bulk of his large estate to his elder daughter Susanna<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[71]</a></sup> under stipulations that she pass it down intact to "the first son of her body".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[72]</a></sup> The Quineys had three children, all of whom died without marrying.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[73]</a></sup> The Halls had one child, Elizabeth, who married twice but died without children in 1670, ending Shakespeare's direct line.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[74]</a></sup> Shakespeare's will scarcely mentions his wife, Anne, who was probably entitled to one-third of his estate automatically.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[75]</a></sup> He did make a point, however, of leaving her "my second best bed", a bequest that has led to much speculation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[76]</a></sup> Some scholars see the bequest as an insult to Anne, whereas others believe that the second-best bed would have been the matrimonial bed and therefore rich in significance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[77]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Shakespeare_grave_-Stratford-upon-Avon_-3June2007.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Shakespeare_grave_-Stratford-upon-Avon_-3June2007.jpg/220px-Shakespeare_grave_-Stratford-upon-Avon_-3June2007.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Shakespeare_grave_-Stratford-upon-Avon_-3June2007.jpg/330px-Shakespeare_grave_-Stratford-upon-Avon_-3June2007.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Shakespeare_grave_-Stratford-upon-Avon_-3June2007.jpg/440px-Shakespeare_grave_-Stratford-upon-Avon_-3June2007.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Shakespeare_grave_-Stratford-upon-Avon_-3June2007.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Shakespeare's grave, next to those of Anne Shakespeare, his wife, and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Nash" title="Thomas Nash">Thomas Nash</a>, the husband of his granddaughter</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Shakespeare was buried in the <a href="/wiki/Chancel" title="Chancel">chancel</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Holy_Trinity_Church,_Stratford-upon-Avon" title="Holy Trinity Church, Stratford-upon-Avon">Holy Trinity Church</a> two days after his death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[78]</a></sup> The epitaph carved into the stone slab covering his grave includes a curse against moving his bones, which was carefully avoided during restoration of the church in 2008:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[79]</a></sup></p>
<div class="poem">
<p><i>Good frend for Iesvs sake forbeare,</i><br/>
<i>To digg the dvst encloased heare.</i><br/>
<i>Bleste be <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Middle_English_the.svg" title="Middle English the.svg"><img alt="Middle English the.svg" data-file-height="19" data-file-width="8" height="19" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Middle_English_the.svg/8px-Middle_English_the.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Middle_English_the.svg/12px-Middle_English_the.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Middle_English_the.svg/16px-Middle_English_the.svg.png 2x" width="8"/></a> man <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Middle_English_that.svg" title="Middle English that.svg"><img alt="Middle English that.svg" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="9" height="20" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Middle_English_that.svg/9px-Middle_English_that.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Middle_English_that.svg/14px-Middle_English_that.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Middle_English_that.svg/18px-Middle_English_that.svg.png 2x" width="9"/></a> spares thes stones,</i><br/>
<i>And cvrst be he <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Middle_English_that.svg" title="Middle English that.svg"><img alt="Middle English that.svg" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="9" height="20" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Middle_English_that.svg/9px-Middle_English_that.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Middle_English_that.svg/14px-Middle_English_that.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Middle_English_that.svg/18px-Middle_English_that.svg.png 2x" width="9"/></a> moves my bones.</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thorn_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-thorn-86">[nb 6]</a></sup></p>
</div>
<p>(Modern spelling: <i>Good friend, for Jesus' sake forbear, / To dig the dust enclosed here. / Blessed be the man that spares these stones, / And cursed be he that moves my bones.</i>)</p>
<p>Sometime before 1623, a <a href="/wiki/Shakespeare%27s_funerary_monument" title="Shakespeare's funerary monument">funerary monument</a> was erected in his memory on the north wall, with a half-effigy of him in the act of writing. Its plaque compares him to <a href="/wiki/Nestor_(mythology)" title="Nestor (mythology)">Nestor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Socrates" title="Socrates">Socrates</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Virgil" title="Virgil">Virgil</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[81]</a></sup> In 1623, in conjunction with the publication of the <a href="/wiki/First_Folio" title="First Folio">First Folio</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Droeshout_engraving" title="Droeshout engraving">Droeshout engraving</a> was published.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[82]</a></sup></p>
<p>Shakespeare has been commemorated in many <a href="/wiki/Memorials_to_William_Shakespeare" title="Memorials to William Shakespeare">statues and memorials</a> around the world, including funeral monuments in <a href="/wiki/Southwark_Cathedral" title="Southwark Cathedral">Southwark Cathedral</a> and <a href="/wiki/Poets%27_Corner" title="Poets' Corner">Poets' Corner</a> in <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Abbey" title="Westminster Abbey">Westminster Abbey</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[84]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Plays">Plays</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Shakespeare%27s_plays" title="Shakespeare's plays">Shakespeare's plays</a> and <a href="/wiki/William_Shakespeare%27s_collaborations" title="William Shakespeare's collaborations">William Shakespeare's collaborations</a></div>
<div class="center">
<div class="thumb tnone">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:502px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Procession_of_Characters_from_Shakespeare%27s_Plays_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1509" data-file-width="6816" height="111" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Procession_of_Characters_from_Shakespeare%27s_Plays_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/500px-Procession_of_Characters_from_Shakespeare%27s_Plays_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Procession_of_Characters_from_Shakespeare%27s_Plays_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/750px-Procession_of_Characters_from_Shakespeare%27s_Plays_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Procession_of_Characters_from_Shakespeare%27s_Plays_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/1000px-Procession_of_Characters_from_Shakespeare%27s_Plays_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" width="500"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Procession_of_Characters_from_Shakespeare%27s_Plays_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Procession of Characters from Shakespeare's Plays</i> by an unknown 19th-century artist</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Most playwrights of the period typically collaborated with others at some point, and critics agree that Shakespeare did the same, mostly early and late in his career.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[85]</a></sup> Some attributions, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Titus_Andronicus" title="Titus Andronicus">Titus Andronicus</a></i> and the early history plays, remain controversial while <i><a href="/wiki/The_Two_Noble_Kinsmen" title="The Two Noble Kinsmen">The Two Noble Kinsmen</a></i> and the lost <i><a href="/wiki/The_History_of_Cardenio" title="The History of Cardenio">Cardenio</a></i> have well-attested contemporary documentation. Textual evidence also supports the view that several of the plays were revised by other writers after their original composition.</p>
<p>The first recorded works of Shakespeare are <i><a href="/wiki/Richard_III_(play)" title="Richard III (play)">Richard III</a></i> and the three parts of <i><a href="/wiki/Henry_VI,_Part_1" title="Henry VI, Part 1">Henry VI</a></i>, written in the early 1590s during a vogue for <a href="/wiki/History_(theatrical_genre)" title="History (theatrical genre)">historical drama</a>. Shakespeare's plays are difficult to date precisely, however,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[86]</a></sup> and studies of the texts suggest that <i>Titus Andronicus</i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Comedy_of_Errors" title="The Comedy of Errors">The Comedy of Errors</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Taming_of_the_Shrew" title="The Taming of the Shrew">The Taming of the Shrew</a>,</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Two_Gentlemen_of_Verona" title="The Two Gentlemen of Verona">The Two Gentlemen of Verona</a></i> may also belong to Shakespeare's earliest period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[87]</a></sup> His first <a href="/wiki/Shakespearean_history" title="Shakespearean history">histories</a>, which draw heavily on the 1587 edition of Raphael Holinshed's <a href="/wiki/Holinshed%27s_Chronicles" title="Holinshed's Chronicles"><i>Chronicles of England, Scotland, and Ireland</i></a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[88]</a></sup> dramatise the destructive results of weak or corrupt rule and have been interpreted as a justification for the origins of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tudor_dynasty" title="Tudor dynasty">Tudor dynasty</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[89]</a></sup> The early plays were influenced by the works of other Elizabethan dramatists, especially <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Kyd" title="Thomas Kyd">Thomas Kyd</a> and <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Marlowe" title="Christopher Marlowe">Christopher Marlowe</a>, by the traditions of medieval drama, and by the plays of <a href="/wiki/Seneca_the_Younger" title="Seneca the Younger">Seneca</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[90]</a></sup> <i>The Comedy of Errors</i> was also based on classical models, but no source for <i>The Taming of the Shrew</i> has been found, though it is related to a separate play of the same name and may have derived from a folk story.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[91]</a></sup> Like <i>The Two Gentlemen of Verona</i>, in which two friends appear to approve of rape,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[92]</a></sup> the <i>Shrew's</i> story of the taming of a woman's independent spirit by a man sometimes troubles modern critics and directors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[93]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Oberon,_Titania_and_Puck_with_Fairies_Dancing._William_Blake._c.1786.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1071" data-file-width="1536" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Oberon%2C_Titania_and_Puck_with_Fairies_Dancing._William_Blake._c.1786.jpg/220px-Oberon%2C_Titania_and_Puck_with_Fairies_Dancing._William_Blake._c.1786.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Oberon%2C_Titania_and_Puck_with_Fairies_Dancing._William_Blake._c.1786.jpg/330px-Oberon%2C_Titania_and_Puck_with_Fairies_Dancing._William_Blake._c.1786.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Oberon%2C_Titania_and_Puck_with_Fairies_Dancing._William_Blake._c.1786.jpg/440px-Oberon%2C_Titania_and_Puck_with_Fairies_Dancing._William_Blake._c.1786.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Oberon,_Titania_and_Puck_with_Fairies_Dancing._William_Blake._c.1786.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Oberon, Titania and Puck with Fairies Dancing</i>. By <a href="/wiki/William_Blake" title="William Blake">William Blake</a>, c. 1786. <a href="/wiki/Tate_Britain" title="Tate Britain">Tate Britain</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Shakespeare's early classical and Italianate comedies, containing tight double plots and precise comic sequences, give way in the mid-1590s to the romantic atmosphere of his most acclaimed comedies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[94]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/A_Midsummer_Night%27s_Dream" title="A Midsummer Night's Dream">A Midsummer Night's Dream</a></i> is a witty mixture of romance, fairy magic, and comic lowlife scenes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[95]</a></sup> Shakespeare's next comedy, the equally romantic <i><a href="/wiki/The_Merchant_of_Venice" title="The Merchant of Venice">Merchant of Venice</a></i>, contains a portrayal of the vengeful Jewish moneylender <a href="/wiki/Shylock" title="Shylock">Shylock</a>, which reflects Elizabethan views but may appear derogatory to modern audiences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[96]</a></sup> The wit and wordplay of <i><a href="/wiki/Much_Ado_About_Nothing" title="Much Ado About Nothing">Much Ado About Nothing</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[97]</a></sup> the charming rural setting of <i><a href="/wiki/As_You_Like_It" title="As You Like It">As You Like It</a></i>, and the lively merrymaking of <i><a href="/wiki/Twelfth_Night" title="Twelfth Night">Twelfth Night</a></i> complete Shakespeare's sequence of great comedies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[98]</a></sup> After the lyrical <i><a href="/wiki/Richard_II_(play)" title="Richard II (play)">Richard II</a></i>, written almost entirely in verse, Shakespeare introduced prose comedy into the histories of the late 1590s, <i><a href="/wiki/Henry_IV,_Part_1" title="Henry IV, Part 1">Henry IV, parts 1</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Henry_IV,_Part_2" title="Henry IV, Part 2">2</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Henry_V_(play)" title="Henry V (play)">Henry V</a></i>. His characters become more complex and tender as he switches deftly between comic and serious scenes, prose and poetry, and achieves the narrative variety of his mature work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[99]</a></sup> This period begins and ends with two tragedies: <i><a href="/wiki/Romeo_and_Juliet" title="Romeo and Juliet">Romeo and Juliet</a></i>, the famous romantic tragedy of sexually charged adolescence, love, and death;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[100]</a></sup> and <i><a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar_(play)" title="Julius Caesar (play)">Julius Caesar</a></i>—based on Sir <a href="/wiki/Thomas_North" title="Thomas North">Thomas North</a>'s 1579 translation of <a href="/wiki/Plutarch" title="Plutarch">Plutarch</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Parallel_Lives" title="Parallel Lives">Parallel Lives</a></i>—which introduced a new kind of drama.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[101]</a></sup> According to Shakespearean scholar James Shapiro, in <i>Julius Caesar,</i> "the various strands of politics, character, inwardness, contemporary events, even Shakespeare's own reflections on the act of writing, began to infuse each other".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[102]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Henry_Fuseli_rendering_of_Hamlet_and_his_father%27s_Ghost.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2024" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Henry_Fuseli_rendering_of_Hamlet_and_his_father%27s_Ghost.JPG/220px-Henry_Fuseli_rendering_of_Hamlet_and_his_father%27s_Ghost.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Henry_Fuseli_rendering_of_Hamlet_and_his_father%27s_Ghost.JPG/330px-Henry_Fuseli_rendering_of_Hamlet_and_his_father%27s_Ghost.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Henry_Fuseli_rendering_of_Hamlet_and_his_father%27s_Ghost.JPG/440px-Henry_Fuseli_rendering_of_Hamlet_and_his_father%27s_Ghost.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Henry_Fuseli_rendering_of_Hamlet_and_his_father%27s_Ghost.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Hamlet, Horatio, Marcellus, and the Ghost of Hamlet's Father</i>. <a href="/wiki/Henry_Fuseli" title="Henry Fuseli">Henry Fuseli</a>, 1780–5. <a href="/wiki/Kunsthaus_Z%C3%BCrich" title="Kunsthaus Zürich">Kunsthaus Zürich</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the early 17th century, Shakespeare wrote the so-called "<a href="/wiki/Shakespearean_problem_play" title="Shakespearean problem play">problem plays</a>" <i><a href="/wiki/Measure_for_Measure" title="Measure for Measure">Measure for Measure</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Troilus_and_Cressida" title="Troilus and Cressida">Troilus and Cressida</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/All%27s_Well_That_Ends_Well" title="All's Well That Ends Well">All's Well That Ends Well</a></i> and a number of his best known <a href="/wiki/Shakespearean_tragedy" title="Shakespearean tragedy">tragedies</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[103]</a></sup> Many critics believe that Shakespeare's greatest tragedies represent the peak of his art. The titular hero of one of Shakespeare's most famous tragedies, <i><a href="/wiki/Hamlet" title="Hamlet">Hamlet</a></i>, has probably been discussed more than any other Shakespearean character, especially for his famous <a href="/wiki/Soliloquy" title="Soliloquy">soliloquy</a> which begins "<a href="/wiki/To_be,_or_not_to_be" title="To be, or not to be">To be or not to be; that is the question</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[104]</a></sup> Unlike the introverted Hamlet, whose fatal flaw is hesitation, the heroes of the tragedies that followed, Othello and King Lear, are undone by hasty errors of judgement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[105]</a></sup> The plots of Shakespeare's tragedies often hinge on such fatal errors or flaws, which overturn order and destroy the hero and those he loves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[106]</a></sup> In <i><a href="/wiki/Othello" title="Othello">Othello</a></i>, the villain <a href="/wiki/Iago" title="Iago">Iago</a> stokes Othello's sexual jealousy to the point where he murders the innocent wife who loves him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[107]</a></sup> In <i><a href="/wiki/King_Lear" title="King Lear">King Lear</a></i>, the old king commits the tragic error of giving up his powers, initiating the events which lead to the torture and blinding of the Earl of Gloucester and the murder of Lear's youngest daughter Cordelia. According to the critic Frank Kermode, "the play offers neither its good characters nor its audience any relief from its cruelty".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[108]</a></sup> In <i><a href="/wiki/Macbeth" title="Macbeth">Macbeth</a></i>, the shortest and most compressed of Shakespeare's tragedies,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[109]</a></sup> uncontrollable ambition incites Macbeth and his wife, <a href="/wiki/Lady_Macbeth" title="Lady Macbeth">Lady Macbeth</a>, to murder the rightful king and usurp the throne until their own guilt destroys them in turn.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[110]</a></sup> In this play, Shakespeare adds a supernatural element to the tragic structure. His last major tragedies, <i><a href="/wiki/Antony_and_Cleopatra" title="Antony and Cleopatra">Antony and Cleopatra</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Coriolanus" title="Coriolanus">Coriolanus</a></i>, contain some of Shakespeare's finest poetry and were considered his most successful tragedies by the poet and critic <a href="/wiki/T._S._Eliot" title="T. S. Eliot">T. S. Eliot</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[111]</a></sup></p>
<p>In his final period, Shakespeare turned to <a href="/wiki/Shakespeare%27s_late_romances" title="Shakespeare's late romances">romance</a> or <a href="/wiki/Tragicomedy" title="Tragicomedy">tragicomedy</a> and completed three more major plays: <i><a href="/wiki/Cymbeline" title="Cymbeline">Cymbeline</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Winter%27s_Tale" title="The Winter's Tale">The Winter's Tale</a>,</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Tempest" title="The Tempest">The Tempest</a></i>, as well as the collaboration, <i><a href="/wiki/Pericles,_Prince_of_Tyre" title="Pericles, Prince of Tyre">Pericles, Prince of Tyre</a></i>. Less bleak than the tragedies, these four plays are graver in tone than the comedies of the 1590s, but they end with reconciliation and the forgiveness of potentially tragic errors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[112]</a></sup> Some commentators have seen this change in mood as evidence of a more serene view of life on Shakespeare's part, but it may merely reflect the theatrical fashion of the day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[113]</a></sup> Shakespeare collaborated on two further surviving plays, <i><a href="/wiki/Henry_VIII_(play)" title="Henry VIII (play)">Henry VIII</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Two_Noble_Kinsmen" title="The Two Noble Kinsmen">The Two Noble Kinsmen</a></i>, probably with <a href="/wiki/John_Fletcher_(playwright)" title="John Fletcher (playwright)">John Fletcher</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[114]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Performances">Performances</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Shakespeare_in_performance" title="Shakespeare in performance">Shakespeare in performance</a></div>
<p>It is not clear for which companies Shakespeare wrote his early plays. The title page of the 1594 edition of <i>Titus Andronicus</i> reveals that the play had been acted by three different troupes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[115]</a></sup> After the <a href="/wiki/Black_Death" title="Black Death">plagues</a> of 1592–3, Shakespeare's plays were performed by his own company at <a href="/wiki/The_Theatre" title="The Theatre">The Theatre</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Curtain_Theatre" title="Curtain Theatre">Curtain</a> in <a href="/wiki/Shoreditch" title="Shoreditch">Shoreditch</a>, north of the Thames.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[116]</a></sup> Londoners flocked there to see the first part of <i>Henry IV</i>, <a href="/wiki/Leonard_Digges_(writer)" title="Leonard Digges (writer)">Leonard Digges</a> recording, "Let but Falstaff come, Hal, Poins, the rest ... and you scarce shall have a room".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[117]</a></sup> When the company found themselves in dispute with their landlord, they pulled The Theatre down and used the timbers to construct the <a href="/wiki/Globe_Theatre" title="Globe Theatre">Globe Theatre</a>, the first playhouse built by actors for actors, on the south bank of the Thames at <a href="/wiki/Southwark" title="Southwark">Southwark</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[118]</a></sup> The Globe opened in autumn 1599, with <i>Julius Caesar</i> one of the first plays staged. Most of Shakespeare's greatest post-1599 plays were written for the Globe, including <i>Hamlet</i>, <i>Othello,</i> and <i>King Lear</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[119]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Shakespeare%C2%B4s_Globe_(8162111781).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1265" data-file-width="1600" height="174" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Shakespeare%C2%B4s_Globe_%288162111781%29.jpg/220px-Shakespeare%C2%B4s_Globe_%288162111781%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Shakespeare%C2%B4s_Globe_%288162111781%29.jpg/330px-Shakespeare%C2%B4s_Globe_%288162111781%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Shakespeare%C2%B4s_Globe_%288162111781%29.jpg/440px-Shakespeare%C2%B4s_Globe_%288162111781%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Shakespeare%C2%B4s_Globe_(8162111781).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The reconstructed <a href="/wiki/Globe_Theatre" title="Globe Theatre">Globe Theatre</a>, London</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After the Lord Chamberlain's Men were renamed the <a href="/wiki/King%27s_Men_(playing_company)" title="King's Men (playing company)">King's Men</a> in 1603, they entered a special relationship with the new <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/James_I_of_England" title="James I of England">King James</a>. Although the performance records are patchy, the King's Men performed seven of Shakespeare's plays at court between 1 November 1604, and 31 October 1605, including two performances of <i>The Merchant of Venice</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[120]</a></sup> After 1608, they performed at the indoor <a href="/wiki/Blackfriars_Theatre" title="Blackfriars Theatre">Blackfriars Theatre</a> during the winter and the Globe during the summer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[121]</a></sup> The indoor setting, combined with the <a href="/wiki/Jacobean_era" title="Jacobean era">Jacobean</a> fashion for lavishly staged <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Masques" title="Masques">masques</a>, allowed Shakespeare to introduce more elaborate stage devices. In <i>Cymbeline</i>, for example, <a href="/wiki/Jupiter_(mythology)" title="Jupiter (mythology)">Jupiter</a> descends "in thunder and lightning, sitting upon an eagle: he throws a thunderbolt. The ghosts fall on their knees."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[122]</a></sup></p>
<p>The actors in Shakespeare's company included the famous <a href="/wiki/Richard_Burbage" title="Richard Burbage">Richard Burbage</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_Kempe" title="William Kempe">William Kempe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Condell" title="Henry Condell">Henry Condell</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Heminges" title="John Heminges">John Heminges</a>. Burbage played the leading role in the first performances of many of Shakespeare's plays, including <i>Richard III</i>, <i>Hamlet</i>, <i>Othello</i>, and <i>King Lear</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[123]</a></sup> The popular comic actor Will Kempe played the servant Peter in <i>Romeo and Juliet</i> and <a href="/wiki/Dogberry" title="Dogberry">Dogberry</a> in <i>Much Ado About Nothing</i>, among other characters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[124]</a></sup> He was replaced around 1600 by <a href="/wiki/Robert_Armin" title="Robert Armin">Robert Armin</a>, who played roles such as <a href="/wiki/Touchstone_(As_You_Like_It)" title="Touchstone (As You Like It)">Touchstone</a> in <i>As You Like It</i> and the fool in <i>King Lear</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[125]</a></sup> In 1613, Sir <a href="/wiki/Henry_Wotton" title="Henry Wotton">Henry Wotton</a> recorded that <i>Henry VIII</i> "was set forth with many extraordinary circumstances of pomp and ceremony".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WellsOxford1247_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-WellsOxford1247-132">[126]</a></sup> On 29 June, however, a cannon set fire to the thatch of the Globe and burned the theatre to the ground, an event which pinpoints the date of a Shakespeare play with rare precision.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WellsOxford1247_132-1"><a href="#cite_note-WellsOxford1247-132">[126]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Textual_sources">Textual sources</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Title_page_William_Shakespeare%27s_First_Folio_1623.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1513" data-file-width="950" height="271" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Title_page_William_Shakespeare%27s_First_Folio_1623.jpg/170px-Title_page_William_Shakespeare%27s_First_Folio_1623.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Title_page_William_Shakespeare%27s_First_Folio_1623.jpg/255px-Title_page_William_Shakespeare%27s_First_Folio_1623.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Title_page_William_Shakespeare%27s_First_Folio_1623.jpg/340px-Title_page_William_Shakespeare%27s_First_Folio_1623.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Title_page_William_Shakespeare%27s_First_Folio_1623.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Title page of the <a href="/wiki/First_Folio" title="First Folio">First Folio</a>, 1623. Copper engraving of Shakespeare by <a href="/wiki/Martin_Droeshout" title="Martin Droeshout">Martin Droeshout</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1623, <a href="/wiki/John_Heminges" title="John Heminges">John Heminges</a> and <a href="/wiki/Henry_Condell" title="Henry Condell">Henry Condell</a>, two of Shakespeare's friends from the King's Men, published the <a href="/wiki/First_Folio" title="First Folio">First Folio</a>, a collected edition of Shakespeare's plays. It contained 36 texts, including 18 printed for the first time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[127]</a></sup> Many of the plays had already appeared in <a href="/wiki/Book_size" title="Book size">quarto</a> versions—flimsy books made from sheets of paper folded twice to make four leaves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Oxfxxxiv_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-Oxfxxxiv-134">[128]</a></sup> No evidence suggests that Shakespeare approved these editions, which the First Folio describes as "stol'n and surreptitious copies".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[129]</a></sup> Nor did Shakespeare plan or expect his works to survive in any form at all; those works likely would have faded into oblivion but for his friends' spontaneous idea, after his death, to create and publish the First Folio.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[130]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Alfred_W._Pollard" title="Alfred W. Pollard">Alfred Pollard</a> termed some of the pre-1623 versions as "<a href="/wiki/Bad_quarto" title="Bad quarto">bad quartos</a>" because of their adapted, paraphrased or garbled texts, which may in places have been reconstructed from memory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[131]</a></sup> Where several versions of a play survive, each <a href="/wiki/Shakespeare%27s_plays#Shakespeare_and_the_textual_problem" title="Shakespeare's plays">differs from the other</a>. The differences may stem from copying or <a href="/wiki/Typesetting#Letterpress_era" title="Typesetting">printing</a> errors, from notes by actors or audience members, or from Shakespeare's own <a href="/wiki/Foul_papers" title="Foul papers">papers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[132]</a></sup> In some cases, for example, <i>Hamlet</i>, <i>Troilus and Cressida,</i> and <i>Othello</i>, Shakespeare could have revised the texts between the quarto and folio editions. In the case of <i><a href="/wiki/King_Lear" title="King Lear">King Lear</a></i>, however, while most modern editions do conflate them, the 1623 folio version is so different from the 1608 quarto that the <i>Oxford Shakespeare</i> prints them both, arguing that they cannot be conflated without confusion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[133]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Poems">Poems</span></h2>
<p>In 1593 and 1594, when the theatres were closed because of <a href="/wiki/Bubonic_plague" title="Bubonic plague">plague</a>, Shakespeare published two narrative poems on sexual themes, <i><a href="/wiki/Venus_and_Adonis_(Shakespeare_poem)" title="Venus and Adonis (Shakespeare poem)">Venus and Adonis</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Rape_of_Lucrece" title="The Rape of Lucrece">The Rape of Lucrece</a></i>. He dedicated them to <a href="/wiki/Henry_Wriothesley,_3rd_Earl_of_Southampton" title="Henry Wriothesley, 3rd Earl of Southampton">Henry Wriothesley, Earl of Southampton</a>. In <i>Venus and Adonis</i>, an innocent <a href="/wiki/Adonis" title="Adonis">Adonis</a> rejects the sexual advances of <a href="/wiki/Venus_(mythology)" title="Venus (mythology)">Venus</a>; while in <i>The Rape of Lucrece</i>, the virtuous wife <a href="/wiki/Lucretia" title="Lucretia">Lucrece</a> is raped by the lustful <a href="/wiki/Sextus_Tarquinius" title="Sextus Tarquinius">Tarquin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[134]</a></sup> Influenced by <a href="/wiki/Ovid" title="Ovid">Ovid</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Metamorphoses" title="Metamorphoses">Metamorphoses</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[135]</a></sup> the poems show the guilt and moral confusion that result from uncontrolled lust.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[136]</a></sup> Both proved popular and were often reprinted during Shakespeare's lifetime. A third narrative poem, <i><a href="/wiki/A_Lover%27s_Complaint" title="A Lover's Complaint">A Lover's Complaint</a></i>, in which a young woman laments her seduction by a persuasive suitor, was printed in the first edition of the <i>Sonnets</i> in 1609. Most scholars now accept that Shakespeare wrote <i>A Lover's Complaint</i>. Critics consider that its fine qualities are marred by leaden effects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[137]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Phoenix_and_the_Turtle" title="The Phoenix and the Turtle">The Phoenix and the Turtle</a></i>, printed in Robert Chester's 1601 <i>Love's Martyr</i>, mourns the deaths of the legendary <a href="/wiki/Phoenix_(mythology)" title="Phoenix (mythology)">phoenix</a> and his lover, the faithful <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Turtle_dove" title="Turtle dove">turtle dove</a>. In 1599, two early drafts of sonnets 138 and 144 appeared in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Passionate_Pilgrim" title="The Passionate Pilgrim">The Passionate Pilgrim</a></i>, published under Shakespeare's name but without his permission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[138]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sonnets">Sonnets</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Shakespeare%27s_sonnets" title="Shakespeare's sonnets">Shakespeare's sonnets</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sonnets1609titlepage.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="1065" height="245" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Sonnets1609titlepage.jpg/170px-Sonnets1609titlepage.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Sonnets1609titlepage.jpg/255px-Sonnets1609titlepage.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Sonnets1609titlepage.jpg/340px-Sonnets1609titlepage.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sonnets1609titlepage.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Title page from 1609 edition of <i>Shake-Speares Sonnets</i></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Published in 1609, the <i><a href="/wiki/Shakespeare%27s_sonnets" title="Shakespeare's sonnets">Sonnets</a></i> were the last of Shakespeare's non-dramatic works to be printed. Scholars are not certain when each of the 154 sonnets was composed, but evidence suggests that Shakespeare wrote sonnets throughout his career for a private readership.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[139]</a></sup> Even before the two unauthorised sonnets appeared in <i>The Passionate Pilgrim</i> in 1599, <a href="/wiki/Francis_Meres" title="Francis Meres">Francis Meres</a> had referred in 1598 to Shakespeare's "sugred Sonnets among his private friends".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[140]</a></sup> Few analysts believe that the published collection follows Shakespeare's intended sequence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[141]</a></sup> He seems to have planned two contrasting series: one about uncontrollable lust for a married woman of dark complexion (the "dark lady"), and one about conflicted love for a fair young man (the "fair youth"). It remains unclear if these figures represent real individuals, or if the authorial "I" who addresses them represents Shakespeare himself, though <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wordsworth" title="Wordsworth">Wordsworth</a> believed that with the sonnets "Shakespeare unlocked his heart".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[142]</a></sup></p>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; padding: 10px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>
<div class="poem">
<p>"Shall I compare thee to a summer's day?<br/>
Thou art more lovely and more temperate ..."</p>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: left;">—Lines from Shakespeare's <i><a href="/wiki/Sonnet_18" title="Sonnet 18">Sonnet 18</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[143]</a></sup></cite></div>
<p>The 1609 edition was dedicated to a "Mr. W.H.", credited as "the only begetter" of the poems. It is not known whether this was written by Shakespeare himself or by the publisher, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Thorpe" title="Thomas Thorpe">Thomas Thorpe</a>, whose initials appear at the foot of the dedication page; nor is it known who Mr. W.H. was, despite numerous theories, or whether Shakespeare even authorised the publication.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[144]</a></sup> Critics praise the <i>Sonnets</i> as a profound meditation on the nature of love, sexual passion, procreation, death, and time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[145]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Style">Style</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shakespeare%27s_style" title="Shakespeare's style">Shakespeare's style</a></div>
<p>Shakespeare's first plays were written in the conventional style of the day. He wrote them in a stylised language that does not always spring naturally from the needs of the characters or the drama.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[146]</a></sup> The poetry depends on extended, sometimes elaborate metaphors and conceits, and the language is often rhetorical—written for actors to declaim rather than speak. The grand speeches in <i><a href="/wiki/Titus_Andronicus" title="Titus Andronicus">Titus Andronicus</a></i>, in the view of some critics, often hold up the action, for example; and the verse in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Two_Gentlemen_of_Verona" title="The Two Gentlemen of Verona">The Two Gentlemen of Verona</a></i> has been described as stilted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[147]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:WilliamBlakePity.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="890" data-file-width="1134" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/WilliamBlakePity.jpeg/220px-WilliamBlakePity.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/WilliamBlakePity.jpeg/330px-WilliamBlakePity.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/WilliamBlakePity.jpeg/440px-WilliamBlakePity.jpeg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:WilliamBlakePity.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i><a href="/wiki/Pity_(William_Blake)" title="Pity (William Blake)">Pity</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/William_Blake" title="William Blake">William Blake</a>, 1795, <a href="/wiki/Tate_Britain" title="Tate Britain">Tate Britain</a>, is an illustration of two similes in <i>Macbeth</i>:
<div class="poem">
<p>"And pity, like a naked new-born babe,<br/>
Striding the blast, or heaven's cherubim, hors'd<br/>
Upon the sightless couriers of the air."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[148]</a></sup></p>
</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>However, Shakespeare soon began to adapt the traditional styles to his own purposes. The opening <a href="/wiki/Soliloquy" title="Soliloquy">soliloquy</a> of <i><a href="/wiki/Richard_III_(play)" title="Richard III (play)">Richard III</a></i> has its roots in the self-declaration of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Vice" title="The Vice">Vice</a> in medieval drama. At the same time, Richard's vivid self-awareness looks forward to the soliloquies of Shakespeare's mature plays.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[149]</a></sup> No single play marks a change from the traditional to the freer style. Shakespeare combined the two throughout his career, with <i><a href="/wiki/Romeo_and_Juliet" title="Romeo and Juliet">Romeo and Juliet</a></i> perhaps the best example of the mixing of the styles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[150]</a></sup> By the time of <i>Romeo and Juliet</i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Richard_II_(play)" title="Richard II (play)">Richard II</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/A_Midsummer_Night%27s_Dream" title="A Midsummer Night's Dream">A Midsummer Night's Dream</a></i> in the mid-1590s, Shakespeare had begun to write a more natural poetry. He increasingly tuned his metaphors and images to the needs of the drama itself.</p>
<p>Shakespeare's standard poetic form was <a href="/wiki/Blank_verse" title="Blank verse">blank verse</a>, composed in <a href="/wiki/Iambic_pentameter" title="Iambic pentameter">iambic pentameter</a>. In practice, this meant that his verse was usually unrhymed and consisted of ten syllables to a line, spoken with a stress on every second syllable. The blank verse of his early plays is quite different from that of his later ones. It is often beautiful, but its sentences tend to start, pause, and finish at the <a href="/wiki/End-stopping" title="End-stopping">end of lines</a>, with the risk of monotony.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[151]</a></sup> Once Shakespeare mastered traditional blank verse, he began to interrupt and vary its flow. This technique releases the new power and flexibility of the poetry in plays such as <i><a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar_(play)" title="Julius Caesar (play)">Julius Caesar</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Hamlet" title="Hamlet">Hamlet</a></i>. Shakespeare uses it, for example, to convey the turmoil in Hamlet's mind:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wright2004p868_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wright2004p868-158">[152]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<div class="poem">
<p>Sir, in my heart there was a kind of fighting<br/>
That would not let me sleep. Methought I lay<br/>
Worse than the mutines in the bilboes. Rashly—<br/>
And prais'd be rashness for it—let us know<br/>
Our indiscretion sometimes serves us well ...</p>
</div>
<div class="templatequotecite">— <cite><i>Hamlet</i>, Act 5, Scene 2, 4–8<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wright2004p868_158-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wright2004p868-158">[152]</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>After <i>Hamlet</i>, Shakespeare varied his poetic style further, particularly in the more emotional passages of the late tragedies. The literary critic <a href="/wiki/A._C._Bradley" title="A. C. Bradley">A. C. Bradley</a> described this style as "more concentrated, rapid, varied, and, in construction, less regular, not seldom twisted or elliptical".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[153]</a></sup> In the last phase of his career, Shakespeare adopted many techniques to achieve these effects.
