These included <a href="/wiki/Enjambment" title="Enjambment">run-on lines</a>, irregular pauses and stops, and extreme variations in sentence structure and length.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McDxxxxii_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-McDxxxxii-160">[154]</a></sup> In <i><a href="/wiki/Macbeth" title="Macbeth">Macbeth</a></i>, for example, the language darts from one unrelated metaphor or simile to another: "was the hope drunk/ Wherein you dressed yourself?" (1.7.35–38); "... pity, like a naked new-born babe/ Striding the blast, or heaven's cherubim, hors'd/ Upon the sightless couriers of the air ..." (1.7.21–25). The listener is challenged to complete the sense.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McDxxxxii_160-1"><a href="#cite_note-McDxxxxii-160">[154]</a></sup> The late romances, with their shifts in time and surprising turns of plot, inspired a last poetic style in which long and short sentences are set against one another, clauses are piled up, subject and object are reversed, and words are omitted, creating an effect of spontaneity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[155]</a></sup></p>
<p>Shakespeare combined poetic genius with a practical sense of the theatre.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[156]</a></sup> Like all playwrights of the time, he dramatised stories from sources such as <a href="/wiki/Plutarch" title="Plutarch">Plutarch</a> and <a href="/wiki/Raphael_Holinshed" title="Raphael Holinshed">Holinshed</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[157]</a></sup> He reshaped each plot to create several centres of interest and to show as many sides of a narrative to the audience as possible. This strength of design ensures that a Shakespeare play can survive translation, cutting and wide interpretation without loss to its core drama.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[158]</a></sup> As Shakespeare's mastery grew, he gave his characters clearer and more varied motivations and distinctive patterns of speech. He preserved aspects of his earlier style in the later plays, however. In <a href="/wiki/Shakespeare%27s_late_romances" title="Shakespeare's late romances">Shakespeare's late romances</a>, he deliberately returned to a more artificial style, which emphasised the illusion of theatre.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[159]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Influence">Influence</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Shakespeare%27s_influence" title="Shakespeare's influence">Shakespeare's influence</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Macbeth_consulting_the_Vision_of_the_Armed_Head.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="645" data-file-width="520" height="211" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Macbeth_consulting_the_Vision_of_the_Armed_Head.jpg/170px-Macbeth_consulting_the_Vision_of_the_Armed_Head.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Macbeth_consulting_the_Vision_of_the_Armed_Head.jpg/255px-Macbeth_consulting_the_Vision_of_the_Armed_Head.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Macbeth_consulting_the_Vision_of_the_Armed_Head.jpg/340px-Macbeth_consulting_the_Vision_of_the_Armed_Head.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Macbeth_consulting_the_Vision_of_the_Armed_Head.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Macbeth Consulting the Vision of the Armed Head</i>. By <a href="/wiki/Henry_Fuseli" title="Henry Fuseli">Henry Fuseli</a>, 1793–94. <a href="/wiki/Folger_Shakespeare_Library" title="Folger Shakespeare Library">Folger Shakespeare Library</a>, Washington.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Shakespeare's work has made a lasting impression on later theatre and literature. In particular, he expanded the dramatic potential of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Characterisation" title="Characterisation">characterisation</a>, plot, <a href="/wiki/Language" title="Language">language</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Genre" title="Genre">genre</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[160]</a></sup> Until <i><a href="/wiki/Romeo_and_Juliet" title="Romeo and Juliet">Romeo and Juliet</a></i>, for example, romance had not been viewed as a worthy topic for tragedy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[161]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Soliloquy" title="Soliloquy">Soliloquies</a> had been used mainly to convey information about characters or events, but Shakespeare used them to explore characters' minds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[162]</a></sup> His work heavily influenced later poetry. The <a href="/wiki/Romanticism" title="Romanticism">Romantic poets</a> attempted to revive Shakespearean verse drama, though with little success. Critic <a href="/wiki/George_Steiner" title="George Steiner">George Steiner</a> described all English verse dramas from <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Taylor_Coleridge" title="Samuel Taylor Coleridge">Coleridge</a> to <a href="/wiki/Alfred,_Lord_Tennyson" title="Alfred, Lord Tennyson">Tennyson</a> as "feeble variations on Shakespearean themes."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[163]</a></sup></p>
<p>Shakespeare influenced novelists such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thomas_Hardy_(writer)" title="Thomas Hardy (writer)">Thomas Hardy</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_Faulkner" title="William Faulkner">William Faulkner</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Charles_Dickens" title="Charles Dickens">Charles Dickens</a>. The American novelist <a href="/wiki/Herman_Melville" title="Herman Melville">Herman Melville</a>'s soliloquies owe much to Shakespeare; his Captain Ahab in <i><a href="/wiki/Moby-Dick" title="Moby-Dick">Moby-Dick</a></i> is a classic <a href="/wiki/Tragic_hero" title="Tragic hero">tragic hero</a>, inspired by <i>King Lear</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[164]</a></sup> Scholars have identified 20,000 pieces of music linked to Shakespeare's works. These include two operas by <a href="/wiki/Giuseppe_Verdi" title="Giuseppe Verdi">Giuseppe Verdi</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Otello" title="Otello">Otello</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Falstaff_(opera)" title="Falstaff (opera)">Falstaff</a></i>, whose critical standing compares with that of the source plays.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[165]</a></sup> Shakespeare has also inspired many painters, including the Romantics and the <a href="/wiki/Pre-Raphaelite_Brotherhood" title="Pre-Raphaelite Brotherhood">Pre-Raphaelites</a>. The Swiss Romantic artist <a href="/wiki/Henry_Fuseli" title="Henry Fuseli">Henry Fuseli</a>, a friend of <a href="/wiki/William_Blake" title="William Blake">William Blake</a>, even translated <i>Macbeth</i> into German.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[166]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Psychoanalyst" title="Psychoanalyst">psychoanalyst</a> <a href="/wiki/Sigmund_Freud" title="Sigmund Freud">Sigmund Freud</a> drew on Shakespearean psychology, in particular, that of Hamlet, for his theories of human nature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[167]</a></sup></p>
<p>In Shakespeare's day, English grammar, spelling, and pronunciation were less standardised than they are now,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECercignani1981_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECercignani1981-174">[168]</a></sup> and his use of language helped shape modern English.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[169]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Johnson" title="Samuel Johnson">Samuel Johnson</a> quoted him more often than any other author in his <i><a href="/wiki/A_Dictionary_of_the_English_Language" title="A Dictionary of the English Language">A Dictionary of the English Language</a></i>, the first serious work of its type.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[170]</a></sup> Expressions such as "with bated breath" (<i>Merchant of Venice</i>) and "a foregone conclusion" (<i>Othello</i>) have found their way into everyday English speech.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[171]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Critical_reputation">Critical reputation</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Shakespeare%27s_reputation" title="Shakespeare's reputation">Shakespeare's reputation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_Shakespeare_criticism" title="Timeline of Shakespeare criticism">Timeline of Shakespeare criticism</a></div>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; padding: 10px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>"He was not of an age, but for all time."</div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: left;">—<a href="/wiki/Ben_Jonson" title="Ben Jonson">Ben Jonson</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[172]</a></sup></cite></div>
<p>Shakespeare was not revered in his lifetime, but he received a large amount of praise.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[173]</a></sup> In 1598, the cleric and author <a href="/wiki/Francis_Meres" title="Francis Meres">Francis Meres</a> singled him out from a group of English writers as "the most excellent" in both comedy and tragedy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[174]</a></sup> The authors of the <i>Parnassus</i> plays at <a href="/wiki/St_John%27s_College,_Cambridge" title="St John's College, Cambridge">St John's College, Cambridge</a> numbered him with <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Chaucer" title="Geoffrey Chaucer">Chaucer</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Gower" title="John Gower">Gower</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Spenser" title="Edmund Spenser">Spenser</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[175]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/First_Folio" title="First Folio">First Folio</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ben_Jonson" title="Ben Jonson">Ben Jonson</a> called Shakespeare the "Soul of the age, the applause, delight, the wonder of our stage", though he had remarked elsewhere that "Shakespeare wanted art".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[176]</a></sup></p>
<p>Between <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Restoration" title="The Restoration">the Restoration</a> of the monarchy in 1660 and the end of the 17th century, classical ideas were in vogue. As a result, critics of the time mostly rated Shakespeare below <a href="/wiki/John_Fletcher_(playwright)" title="John Fletcher (playwright)">John Fletcher</a> and Ben Jonson.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[177]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Rymer" title="Thomas Rymer">Thomas Rymer</a>, for example, condemned Shakespeare for mixing the comic with the tragic. Nevertheless, poet and critic <a href="/wiki/John_Dryden" title="John Dryden">John Dryden</a> rated Shakespeare highly, saying of Jonson, "I admire him, but I love Shakespeare".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[178]</a></sup> For several decades, Rymer's view held sway; but during the 18th century, critics began to respond to Shakespeare on his own terms and acclaim what they termed his natural genius. A series of scholarly editions of his work, notably those of <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Johnson" title="Samuel Johnson">Samuel Johnson</a> in 1765 and <a href="/wiki/Edmond_Malone" title="Edmond Malone">Edmond Malone</a> in 1790, added to his growing reputation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[179]</a></sup> By 1800, he was firmly enshrined as the national poet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[180]</a></sup> In the 18th and 19th centuries, his reputation also spread abroad. Among those who championed him were the writers <a href="/wiki/Voltaire" title="Voltaire">Voltaire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Johann_Wolfgang_von_Goethe" title="Johann Wolfgang von Goethe">Goethe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stendhal" title="Stendhal">Stendhal</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Victor_Hugo" title="Victor Hugo">Victor Hugo</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[181]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:William_Shakespeare_Statue_in_Lincoln_Park.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2288" data-file-width="1289" height="302" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/William_Shakespeare_Statue_in_Lincoln_Park.JPG/170px-William_Shakespeare_Statue_in_Lincoln_Park.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/William_Shakespeare_Statue_in_Lincoln_Park.JPG/255px-William_Shakespeare_Statue_in_Lincoln_Park.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/William_Shakespeare_Statue_in_Lincoln_Park.JPG/340px-William_Shakespeare_Statue_in_Lincoln_Park.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:William_Shakespeare_Statue_in_Lincoln_Park.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A recently garlanded statue of William Shakespeare in <a href="/wiki/Lincoln_Park,_Chicago" title="Lincoln Park, Chicago">Lincoln Park, Chicago</a>, typical of many created in the 19th and early 20th century</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During the <a href="/wiki/Romanticism" title="Romanticism">Romantic era</a>, Shakespeare was praised by the poet and literary philosopher <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Taylor_Coleridge" title="Samuel Taylor Coleridge">Samuel Taylor Coleridge</a>; and the critic <a href="/wiki/August_Wilhelm_Schlegel" title="August Wilhelm Schlegel">August Wilhelm Schlegel</a> translated his plays in the spirit of <a href="/wiki/German_Romanticism" title="German Romanticism">German Romanticism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[182]</a></sup> In the 19th century, critical admiration for Shakespeare's genius often bordered on adulation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[183]</a></sup> "That King Shakespeare," the essayist <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Carlyle" title="Thomas Carlyle">Thomas Carlyle</a> wrote in 1840, "does not he shine, in crowned sovereignty, over us all, as the noblest, gentlest, yet strongest of rallying signs; indestructible".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[184]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Victorian_era" title="Victorian era">Victorians</a> produced his plays as lavish spectacles on a grand scale.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[185]</a></sup> The playwright and critic <a href="/wiki/George_Bernard_Shaw" title="George Bernard Shaw">George Bernard Shaw</a> mocked the cult of Shakespeare worship as "<a href="/wiki/Bardolatry" title="Bardolatry">bardolatry</a>", claiming that the new <a href="/wiki/Naturalism_(theatre)" title="Naturalism (theatre)">naturalism</a> of <a href="/wiki/Henrik_Ibsen" title="Henrik Ibsen">Ibsen's</a> plays had made Shakespeare obsolete.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[186]</a></sup></p>
<p>The modernist revolution in the arts during the early 20th century, far from discarding Shakespeare, eagerly enlisted his work in the service of the <a href="/wiki/Avant-garde" title="Avant-garde">avant-garde</a>. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/German_expressionism" title="German expressionism">Expressionists in Germany</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Futurism" title="Futurism">Futurists</a> in Moscow mounted productions of his plays. Marxist playwright and director <a href="/wiki/Bertolt_Brecht" title="Bertolt Brecht">Bertolt Brecht</a> devised an <a href="/wiki/Epic_theatre" title="Epic theatre">epic theatre</a> under the influence of Shakespeare. The poet and critic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/T.S._Eliot" title="T.S. Eliot">T.S. Eliot</a> argued against Shaw that Shakespeare's "primitiveness" in fact made him truly modern.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[187]</a></sup> Eliot, along with <a href="/wiki/G._Wilson_Knight" title="G. Wilson Knight">G. Wilson Knight</a> and the school of <a href="/wiki/New_Criticism" title="New Criticism">New Criticism</a>, led a movement towards a closer reading of Shakespeare's imagery. In the 1950s, a wave of new critical approaches replaced modernism and paved the way for "<a href="/wiki/Postmodernism" title="Postmodernism">post-modern</a>" studies of Shakespeare.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[188]</a></sup> By the 1980s, Shakespeare studies were open to movements such as <a href="/wiki/Structuralism" title="Structuralism">structuralism</a>, feminism, <a href="/wiki/New_Historicism" title="New Historicism">New Historicism</a>, <a href="/wiki/African-American_studies" title="African-American studies">African-American studies</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Queer_studies" title="Queer studies">queer studies</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[189]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[190]</a></sup> In a comprehensive reading of Shakespeare's works and comparing Shakespeare literary accomplishments to accomplishments among leading figures in philosophy and theology as well, Harold Bloom has commented that "Shakespeare was larger than Plato and than St. Augustine. He <i>encloses</i> us because we <i>see</i> with his fundamental perceptions."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[191]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Works">Works</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Shakespeare_bibliography" title="Shakespeare bibliography">Shakespeare bibliography</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chronology_of_Shakespeare%27s_plays" title="Chronology of Shakespeare's plays">Chronology of Shakespeare's plays</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification_of_the_plays">Classification of the plays</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gilbert_WShakespeares_Plays.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2040" data-file-width="2595" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Gilbert_WShakespeares_Plays.jpg/220px-Gilbert_WShakespeares_Plays.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Gilbert_WShakespeares_Plays.jpg/330px-Gilbert_WShakespeares_Plays.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Gilbert_WShakespeares_Plays.jpg/440px-Gilbert_WShakespeares_Plays.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gilbert_WShakespeares_Plays.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>The Plays of William Shakespeare</i>. By <a href="/wiki/John_Gilbert_(painter)" title="John Gilbert (painter)">Sir John Gilbert</a>, 1849.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Shakespeare's works include the 36 plays printed in the <a href="/wiki/First_Folio" title="First Folio">First Folio</a> of 1623, listed according to their folio classification as <a href="/wiki/Shakespearean_comedy" title="Shakespearean comedy">comedies</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shakespearean_history" title="Shakespearean history">histories</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Shakespearean_tragedy" title="Shakespearean tragedy">tragedies</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[192]</a></sup> Two plays not included in the First Folio, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Two_Noble_Kinsmen" title="The Two Noble Kinsmen">The Two Noble Kinsmen</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Pericles,_Prince_of_Tyre" title="Pericles, Prince of Tyre">Pericles, Prince of Tyre</a></i>, are now accepted as part of the canon, with today's scholars agreeing that Shakespeare made major contributions to the writing of both.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[193]</a></sup> No Shakespearean poems were included in the First Folio.</p>
<p>In the late 19th century, <a href="/wiki/Edward_Dowden" title="Edward Dowden">Edward Dowden</a> classified four of the late comedies as <a href="/wiki/Shakespeare%27s_late_romances" title="Shakespeare's late romances">romances</a>, and though many scholars prefer to call them <i><a href="/wiki/Tragicomedy" title="Tragicomedy">tragicomedies</a></i>, Dowden's term is often used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[194]</a></sup> In 1896, <a href="/wiki/Frederick_S._Boas" title="Frederick S. Boas">Frederick S. Boas</a> coined the term "<a href="/wiki/Shakespearean_problem_play" title="Shakespearean problem play">problem plays</a>" to describe four plays: <i><a href="/wiki/All%27s_Well_That_Ends_Well" title="All's Well That Ends Well">All's Well That Ends Well</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Measure_for_Measure" title="Measure for Measure">Measure for Measure</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Troilus_and_Cressida" title="Troilus and Cressida">Troilus and Cressida</a>,</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Hamlet" title="Hamlet">Hamlet</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[195]</a></sup> "Dramas as singular in theme and temper cannot be strictly called comedies or tragedies", he wrote. "We may, therefore, borrow a convenient phrase from the theatre of today and class them together as Shakespeare's problem plays."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[196]</a></sup> The term, much debated and sometimes applied to other plays, remains in use, though <i>Hamlet</i> is definitively classed as a tragedy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[197]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Speculation_about_Shakespeare">Speculation about Shakespeare</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Authorship">Authorship</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Shakespeare_authorship_question" title="Shakespeare authorship question">Shakespeare authorship question</a></div>
<p>Around 230 years after Shakespeare's death, doubts began to be expressed about the authorship of the works attributed to him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[198]</a></sup> Proposed alternative candidates include <a href="/wiki/Francis_Bacon" title="Francis Bacon">Francis Bacon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Marlowe" title="Christopher Marlowe">Christopher Marlowe</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Edward_de_Vere,_17th_Earl_of_Oxford" title="Edward de Vere, 17th Earl of Oxford">Edward de Vere, 17th Earl of Oxford</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[199]</a></sup> Several "group theories" have also been proposed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[200]</a></sup> Only a small minority of academics believe there is reason to question the traditional attribution,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[201]</a></sup> but interest in the subject, particularly the <a href="/wiki/Oxfordian_theory_of_Shakespeare_authorship" title="Oxfordian theory of Shakespeare authorship">Oxfordian theory of Shakespeare authorship</a>, continues into the 21st century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[202]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Religious_views_of_William_Shakespeare" title="Religious views of William Shakespeare">Religious views of William Shakespeare</a></div>
<p>Some scholars claim that members of Shakespeare's family were Catholics, at a time when practicing Catholicism in England was against the law.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[203]</a></sup> Shakespeare's mother, <a href="/wiki/Mary_Shakespeare" title="Mary Shakespeare">Mary Arden</a>, certainly came from a pious Catholic family. The strongest evidence might be a Catholic statement of faith signed by his father, <a href="/wiki/John_Shakespeare" title="John Shakespeare">John Shakespeare</a>, found in 1757 in the rafters of his former house in Henley Street. However, the document is now lost and scholars differ as to its authenticity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[204]</a></sup> In 1591, the authorities reported that John Shakespeare had missed church "for fear of process for debt", a common Catholic excuse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cath_211-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cath-211">[205]</a></sup> In 1606, the name of William's daughter Susanna appears on a list of those who failed to attend Easter <a href="/wiki/Eucharist" title="Eucharist">communion</a> in Stratford.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cath_211-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cath-211">[205]</a></sup> As several scholars have noted, whatever his private views, Shakespeare "conformed to the official state religion", as <a href="/wiki/Park_Honan" title="Park Honan">Park Honan</a> put it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212"><a href="#cite_note-212">[206]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[207]</a></sup> Also, <a href="/wiki/Shakespeare%27s_will" title="Shakespeare's will">Shakespeare's will</a> uses a Protestant formula, and he was a confirmed member of the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a>, where he was married, his children were baptized, and where he is buried. Other authors argue that there is a lack of evidence about Shakespeare's religious beliefs. Scholars find evidence both for and against Shakespeare's Catholicism, Protestantism, or lack of belief in his plays, but the truth may be impossible to prove.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[208]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sexuality">Sexuality</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sexuality_of_William_Shakespeare" title="Sexuality of William Shakespeare">Sexuality of William Shakespeare</a></div>
<p>Few details of Shakespeare's sexuality are known. At 18, he married 26-year-old <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anne_Hathaway_(Shakespeare%27s_wife)" title="Anne Hathaway (Shakespeare's wife)">Anne Hathaway</a>, who was pregnant. Susanna, the first of their three children, was born six months later on 26 May 1583. Over the centuries, some readers have posited that Shakespeare's sonnets are autobiographical,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-215"><a href="#cite_note-215">[209]</a></sup> and point to them as evidence of his love for a young man. Others read the same passages as the expression of intense friendship rather than romantic love.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216"><a href="#cite_note-216">[210]</a></sup> The 26 so-called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Dark_Lady" title="The Dark Lady">"Dark Lady"</a> sonnets, addressed to a married woman, are taken as evidence of heterosexual liaisons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217"><a href="#cite_note-217">[211]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Portraiture">Portraiture</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Portraits_of_Shakespeare" title="Portraits of Shakespeare">Portraits of Shakespeare</a></div>
<p>No written contemporary description of Shakespeare's physical appearance survives, and no evidence suggests that he ever commissioned a portrait, so the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Droeshout_engraving" title="Droeshout engraving">Droeshout engraving</a>, which <a href="/wiki/Ben_Jonson" title="Ben Jonson">Ben Jonson</a> approved of as a good likeness,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-218"><a href="#cite_note-218">[212]</a></sup> and his <a href="/wiki/Shakespeare%27s_funerary_monument" title="Shakespeare's funerary monument">Stratford monument</a> provide perhaps the best evidence of his appearance. From the 18th century, the desire for authentic Shakespeare portraits fuelled claims that various surviving pictures depicted Shakespeare. That demand also led to the production of several fake portraits, as well as misattributions, repaintings, and relabelling of portraits of other people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219"><a href="#cite_note-219">[213]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Footnotes">Footnotes</span></h3>
<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-dates-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-dates_2-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Dates follow the <a href="/wiki/Julian_calendar" title="Julian calendar">Julian calendar</a>, used in England throughout Shakespeare's lifespan, but with the start of the year adjusted to 1 January (see <a href="/wiki/Old_Style_and_New_Style_dates" title="Old Style and New Style dates">Old Style and New Style dates</a>). Under the <a href="/wiki/Gregorian_calendar" title="Gregorian calendar">Gregorian calendar</a>, adopted in Catholic countries in 1582, Shakespeare died on 3 May (<a href="#CITEREFSchoenbaum1987">Schoenbaum 1987</a>, xv).</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-national-cult-5"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-national-cult_5-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The "national cult" of Shakespeare, and the "bard" identification, dates from September 1769, when the actor <a href="/wiki/David_Garrick" title="David Garrick">David Garrick</a> organised a week-long carnival at Stratford to mark the town council awarding him the <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_the_City" title="Freedom of the City">freedom</a> of the town. In addition to presenting the town with a statue of Shakespeare, Garrick composed a doggerel verse, lampooned in the London newspapers, naming the banks of the Avon as the birthplace of the "matchless Bard" (<a href="#CITEREFMcIntyre1999">McIntyre 1999</a>, 412–432).</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-exact-figures-6"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-exact-figures_6-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The exact figures are unknown. See <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shakespeare%27s_collaborations" title="Shakespeare's collaborations">Shakespeare's collaborations</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shakespeare_Apocrypha" title="Shakespeare Apocrypha">Shakespeare Apocrypha</a> for further details.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-play-dates-10"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-play-dates_10-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Individual play dates and precise writing span are unknown. See <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chronology_of_William_Shakespeare%27s_plays" title="Chronology of William Shakespeare's plays">Chronology of Shakespeare's plays</a> for further details.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-25"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-25">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The <a href="/wiki/Crest_(heraldry)" title="Crest (heraldry)">crest</a> is a silver falcon supporting a spear, while the motto is <i>Non Sanz Droict</i> (French for "not without right"). This motto is still used by <a href="/wiki/Warwickshire_County_Council" title="Warwickshire County Council">Warwickshire County Council</a>, in reference to Shakespeare.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-thorn-86"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-thorn_86-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In the scribal abbreviations <i>ye</i> for <i>the</i> (3rd line) and <i>yt</i> for <i>that</i> (3rd and 4th lines) the letter <i>y</i> represents in fact <i>th</i>: see article <i><a href="/wiki/Thorn_(letter)" title="Thorn (letter)">thorn</a></i>.</span></li>
</ol>
</div>

<td class="navbox-list navbox-odd" colspan="2" style="width:100%;padding:0px">

<th class="navbox-group" scope="row" style="width:1%">Opera and<br/>
ballet adaptations</th>
<td class="navbox-list navbox-odd hlist" style="text-align:left;border-left-width:2px;border-left-style:solid;width:100%;padding:0px">
<div style="padding:0em 0.25em">

<th class="navbox-group" scope="row" style="width:1%"><i><a href="/wiki/Henry_VI,_Part_1#Characters" title="Henry VI, Part 1">1 Henry VI</a></i></th>
<td class="navbox-list navbox-odd" style="text-align:left;border-left-width:2px;border-left-style:solid;width:100%;padding:0px">
